    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_252942980" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/252942980" />
    <a title="Mir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/237469-mir"><img alt="Mir" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1275489063p2/237469.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/252942980?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mir" href="/user/show/237469-mir">Mir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer252942980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer816138039294420219"><br/>The Leopard. <br/>One of the four "big cats," it is a fierce predator: fast, voracious, strong enough to crush a skull with its jaws and to drag an animal almost as heavy as itself into a tree. Fearsome.<br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380365866i/718007._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
 <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br/>Like most felines, the leopard expends energy in massive bursts and must sleep for the most of the day to recoup its strength for the hunt. Do these long stretches of dormancy make the leopard lazy? Would it, free from the demands of hunger, wile away day after day i</span>
  <span id="freeText816138039294420219" style="display:none"><br>The Leopard. <br>One of the four "big cats," it is a fierce predator: fast, voracious, strong enough to crush a skull with its jaws and to drag an animal almost as heavy as itself into a tree. Fearsome.<br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380365866i/718007._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br>Like most felines, the leopard expends energy in massive bursts and must sleep for the most of the day to recoup its strength for the hunt. Do these long stretches of dormancy make the leopard lazy? Would it, free from the demands of hunger, wile away day after day in slumber? Of course not. Anyone who has visited a zoo and witnessed the miserable, restless pacing of the great cats knows that they crave physical action and mental stimulation as much as they crave food and rest. <br><br>Humans may be better at accustoming themselves to inactivity, distracting themselves from the emptiness of their lives with petty, time-filling concerns, but we too crave meaningful activity. The ideal for the Roman gentleman was <i>otium</i> -- leisure. But not an empty, inert leisure; <i>otium</i> was rather the "leisure" of having free time to read, write, and converse intelligently with friends about intellectually stimulating topics. It was assumed that <i>otium</i> would be balanced by the substantial demands of <i>negotiis</i>, the daily demands of business, home, political life, et alia. Larger amounts of <i>otium</i> might be the dutiful Roman's reward after a long career of military or civil service. <br><br>In Lampedusa's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/625094.The_Leopard" title="The Leopard by Giuseppe Tomasi di Lampedusa" rel="nofollow noopener">The Leopard</a> we see what happens when the idealization of leisure is retained without the demands of work and duty: neglect, stagnation, decay. Emptied of purpose and meaning, life becomes empty of pleasure as well, losing its savor when it no longer has has limits or contrasts. <br><br><i>Otio qui nescit uti . . .</i></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="816138039294420219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_90908200" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/90908200" />
    <a title="J" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/731690-j"><img alt="J" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1288001880p2/731690.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/90908200?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J" href="/user/show/731690-j">J</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375714795" href="/book/show/2193815.The_Leopard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/731690-j?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer90908200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13594014006841156079">I. Nunc, et in hora mortis nostrae. Now, and in the hour of our death. Amen.<br/><br/>Thus begins Lampedusa’s masterpiece, his paean to death. Sensuous, insightful, subtle, <i>The Leopard</i> is a work of absolute beauty. <br/><br/>In 1860 Don Fabrizio, Prince of Salina, is watching the lifeblood seep from his world: the power and the prestige, the unquestioned honors are all fading away, being bled out by revolution. He simply watches it go. He is resigned to it as he is resigned to his own nature. Sated ease tinged with</span>
  <span id="freeText13594014006841156079" style="display:none">I. Nunc, et in hora mortis nostrae. Now, and in the hour of our death. Amen.<br /><br />Thus begins Lampedusa’s masterpiece, his paean to death. Sensuous, insightful, subtle, <i>The Leopard</i> is a work of absolute beauty. <br /><br />In 1860 Don Fabrizio, Prince of Salina, is watching the lifeblood seep from his world: the power and the prestige, the unquestioned honors are all fading away, being bled out by revolution. He simply watches it go. He is resigned to it as he is resigned to his own nature. Sated ease tinged with disgust. His one constant joy in life, where he can escape this sense of himself, is the stars. He’s an astronomer and in the sky he finds blissful anonymity. There is no false revolution there. In the limitlessness of the sky there are no worries, only the reassurance that none of the rest matters because nothing ever really changes.<br /><br />II. The history is interesting, but it’s superficial. E. M. Forster said <i>The Leopard</i> is not a historical novel, but a novel which happens to take place in history. The real story is something else. Somewhere between the characters – drawn too well to be forgotten – and the very fiber of Sicilians themselves.<br /><br /><i>All Sicilian expression, even the most violent, is really wish-fulfillment: our sensuality is a hankering for oblivion, our shooting and knifing a hankering for death; our languor, our exotic vices, a hankering for voluptuous immobility, that is for death again.</i><br /><br />Under this definition, I know a number of Sicilians who’ve never set foot in Sicily.<br /><br /><i>Perhaps only Tancredi had understood for an instant, when he had said with that subdued irony of his, “Uncle, you are courting death.”</i> Don Fabrizio looks for her constantly, sniffing the air for her scent, expecting to find her at every turn. This yearning for oblivion is so strong it’s a tangible thing. Fingering the rosary beads in the very first sentence. <i>Nunc, et in hora mortis nostrae.</i> Young lovers discovering ways of satisfying dark desires lost to the consciousness; beneath the conscious, the house, the gardens, the very air exhales it. Blackmail through beauty, redemption through blood. A dying man: <i>A long open wagon came by stacked with bulls killed shortly before at the slaughterhouse, already quartered and exhibiting their intimate mechanism with the shamelessness of death. At intervals a big thick red drop fell onto the pavement. At a crossroad he glimpsed the sky to the west, above the sea. There was Venus, wrapped in her turban of autumn mist. She was always faithful, always waiting for Don Fabrizio on his early morning outings, at Donnafugata before a shoot, now after a ball. Don Fabrizio sighed. When would she decide to give him an appointment less ephemeral, far from carcasses and blood, in her own region of perennial certitude?</i><br /><br />III. Floating above this longing for oblivion there is a story. Parts of it parallel the politics of Italy. Parts of it made my heart ache. There are sly bits that made me laugh. <br /><br /><i>Restless and domineering, the Princess dropped her rosary brusquely into her jet-fringed bag, while her fine crazy eyes glanced around at her slaves of children and her tyrant of a husband, over whom her diminutive body vainly yearned for loving dominion.</i><br /><br />IV. Finally, let’s start at the beginning. From the back cover: Set in the 1860s, <i>The Leopard</i> tells the spellbinding story of a decadent, dying Sicilian aristocracy threatened by the approaching forces of democracy and revolution. The dramatic sweep and richness of observation, the seamless intertwining of public and private worlds, and the grasp of human frailty imbue <i>The Leopard</i> with its particular melancholy beauty and power, and place it among the greatest historical novels of our time.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13594014006841156079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_250268512" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/250268512" />
    <a title="Eric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26852-eric"><img alt="Eric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383287017p2/26852.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/250268512?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eric" href="/user/show/26852-eric">Eric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780394756684" href="/book/show/2503920.The_Leopard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26852-eric?shelf=ficciones">ficciones</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26852-eric?shelf=decadence">decadence</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26852-eric?shelf=historiophantasmagoria">historiophantasmagoria</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer250268512" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17006313458211440406">What complaints I have about <i>The Leopard</i> are minutely stylistic; and because "to present any writer in translation is to present him bereft of his style," as Clarence Brown, one of Mandelstam’s English avatars, reminds us, I won’t dwell on the elaborate clunkiness and awkward extensions of Lampedusa’s metaphors, especially those applied to the inner emotional states of his characters. In Italian this figurative language may be impossibly smooth. <br/><br/>What I love in this novel is its morbid and pessim</span>
  <span id="freeText17006313458211440406" style="display:none">What complaints I have about <i>The Leopard</i> are minutely stylistic; and because "to present any writer in translation is to present him bereft of his style," as Clarence Brown, one of Mandelstam’s English avatars, reminds us, I won’t dwell on the elaborate clunkiness and awkward extensions of Lampedusa’s metaphors, especially those applied to the inner emotional states of his characters. In Italian this figurative language may be impossibly smooth. <br /><br />What I love in this novel is its morbid and pessimistic sensuality, its Gotho-Baudelairean heritage. The Prince’s garden, where his dog revels in “dead lizards and manure,” exhales a miasma of “cloying, fleshy, and slightly putrid” scents; the cuttings of roses, brought by the Prince from Paris, in Sicily’s climate and soil yield what look like “flesh-colored cabbages”; moist, heavy, pudendal — "The Prince put one under his nose and seemed to be sniffing the thigh of a dancer from the Opera.” Sicily having become a civic battleground between the Bourbon dynasty and nationalist rebels, some time passes before anyone is able to distinguish the odor of a decomposing soldier from the garden’s riot of other sweet stinks. The high point for me is when the Prince’s nephew, Tancredi, and his fiancé Angelica roam the fairy tale labyrinthine reaches of the dilapidated palace at Donnafugata. Having eluded their chaperones, and watched only by “a shepherdess glancing down consenting from some obliterated fresco,” the two one day enter a hidden door into an enigmatic suite of rooms — well, enigmatic to their innocence — all floored with white marble sloping away toward gutters. There are mirrors across the walls (some smashed) and “wide, too wide” sofas (“showing nails with traces of silk that had been torn away”), and on the fireplaces “delicate little marble intaglios, naked figures in paroxysms...mutilated by some furious hammer.” The libertine's elegant dungeon, though defaced and sealed by horrified descendants, still yields up a bundle of small whips to the lovers’ snooping—and they discover still other whips, in the wing consecrated to the memory of a pious ancestor who flagellated his sinful flesh. I laughed so loud. A lineage of lashes — sign of “the human ‘always’” the Prince thinks of when an official boasts of the efficiency and modernity the new regime will bring to Sicily. The Salinas, when libertine, beat others; when pious, they beat themselves — the ancestral palace a “universe of extinct vices, forgotten virtues, and above all, perennial desire.” <br /><br />But I don’t mean to give the impression that <i>The Leopard</i> is a Surrealist shriek or a Decadent’s delirium. The really febrile elements are held at an distance, in the stately realist integrity of a historical novel that also concerns itself with democracy and nationalism, clerical disestablishment and the rise of the middle classes. Lampedusa’s external biography — an apparently idle Sicilian aristocrat, fantastically erudite, mariolatrous and homebound à la Borges, who read and waited and read some more, finally undertaking his only novel at age fifty-seven — perfectly answers the curious, compounded, involuted feel of <i>The Leopard</i>, its air of something dreamt in its particulars long before ordered as a narrative.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17006313458211440406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1434669710" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1434669710" />
    <a title="Barry Pierce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4593541-barry-pierce"><img alt="Barry Pierce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588975731p2/4593541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1434669710?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barry Pierce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barry Pierce" href="/user/show/4593541-barry-pierce">Barry Pierce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099512158" href="/book/show/1320341.The_Leopard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4593541-barry-pierce?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4593541-barry-pierce?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1434669710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10974965144744333139">What do I think of this? On one hand I want to laud it as being a classic of Italian literature, imbued with the essence of Lermontov and breadth of Tolstoy. However, on the other hand, this is essentially just one long episode of <i>Downton Abbey</i> but with less Maggie Smith and more Garibaldi. I'm conflicted about this one a bit because it does have some <i>really</i> boring parts but then it has some just magical passages. Eh, I liked it, but it's barely clinging on to those three stars. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2380706923" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2380706923" />
    <a title="Gabrielle Dubois" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28233498-gabrielle-dubois"><img alt="Gabrielle Dubois" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510402879p2/28233498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2380706923?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle Dubois" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle Dubois" href="/user/show/28233498-gabrielle-dubois">Gabrielle Dubois</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28233498-gabrielle-dubois?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2380706923" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1644368808087787074">In Italy, in 1860, a decadent and impoverished aristocracy, deaf to the upheavals of the world, still reigns over Sicily. But the disembarkation of the troops of the republican Giuseppe Garibaldi who wants to reunify Italy divided into several kingdoms, initiates the overthrow of a secular social order. We enter the intimacy of the thought and the life of Don Fabrizio Corbera, Prince of Salina, great landowner, aware of the threat of disappearance that hangs over his caste and his family, but wh</span>
  <span id="freeText1644368808087787074" style="display:none">In Italy, in 1860, a decadent and impoverished aristocracy, deaf to the upheavals of the world, still reigns over Sicily. But the disembarkation of the troops of the republican Giuseppe Garibaldi who wants to reunify Italy divided into several kingdoms, initiates the overthrow of a secular social order. We enter the intimacy of the thought and the life of Don Fabrizio Corbera, Prince of Salina, great landowner, aware of the threat of disappearance that hangs over his caste and his family, but who thinks more than he acts, unlike his nephew, the handsome, clever and lively Tancredi, who will fight alongside Garibaldi after announcing to his uncle and guardian:<br />"If we want everything to remain as it is, everything has to change."<br /><br />I had seen in my youth, as the old sages say! the wonderful Visconti film from this novel, with Burt Lancaster, Claudia Cardinale and Alain Delon.<br />But the book is wonderfully written by Giuseppe Tomasi di Lampedusa. It is built, limpid and deep.<br />From the first page, although many things happen, in the thought of the Prince, his actions and those of the characters around him, the pace adopts a torpor as heavy as the land of Sicily, as dry as its air, as overwhelming and blinding as its burning sun.<br /><br />First excerpt to make you want to read this masterpiece:<br />One night, the Prince descends from his property to the city of Palermo. In this very Catholic Italy, landscape, countryside and cities are dotted with monasteries and convents of all orders. In the surrounding mountains, the revolutionaries of Garibaldi are organizing themselves:<br />"Soon it would be dark, the convents and monasteries became the despots of the panorama. It was against them, in fact, that the fires in the mountains were kindled, fanned by men who were entirely like those who lived in convents, equally fanatical, equally closed-minded, just as eager to power, that is to say, as usual, idleness."<br /><br />Second excerpt showing the fine writing of Lampedusa:<br />"Although the Administration was the place where the greatest trivialities were accomplished, its appearance was a severe austerity. "<br /><br />Third excerpt, the Prince talks to Father Pirrone:<br />"We are not blind. We live in a mobile reality to which we seek to adapt as seaweeds bend under the pressure of the sea. Immortality has been promised to the Holy Church; to us, as a social class, it hasn’t. For us, a palliative that promises to last a hundred years is equivalent to eternity."<br /><br />Fourth excerpt: Men go by, societies crumble, powers change hands, but the world remains basically the same:<br />"The great lords were reserved and incomprehensible; the peasants were explicit and clear; but the Demon deceived them all the same way."<br /><br />PS: I read this book in French, and quotes are translated by myself, so don't judge Lampedusa's writing by reading them!</span>
  <a data-text-id="1644368808087787074" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2383661164" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2383661164" />
    <a title="Alice Poon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon"><img alt="Alice Poon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499099841p2/28700577.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2383661164?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice Poon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice Poon" href="/user/show/28700577-alice-poon">Alice Poon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28700577-alice-poon?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2383661164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1964628621349879757">I had never read anything about the history of Italy’s unification (called the “Risorgimento”), and was glad to read this charming novel. The story is set in 1860s Sicily and accounts for the personal trajectory of a Sicilian aristocrat Fabrizio Salina as he gets caught up in the social and political storm that would bring democracy and irrevocable changes to the various disparate Italian states.<br/><br/>Written in a lush style embellished by similes and metaphors, the story is told through a narrator wh</span>
  <span id="freeText1964628621349879757" style="display:none">I had never read anything about the history of Italy’s unification (called the “Risorgimento”), and was glad to read this charming novel. The story is set in 1860s Sicily and accounts for the personal trajectory of a Sicilian aristocrat Fabrizio Salina as he gets caught up in the social and political storm that would bring democracy and irrevocable changes to the various disparate Italian states.<br /><br />Written in a lush style embellished by similes and metaphors, the story is told through a narrator whose voice is tinged at once with nostalgic melancholy and dry humor, chiefly from Fabrizio Salina’s viewpoint. In face of imminent upheavals instigated by revolutionaries led by Giuseppe Garibaldi who aims at uniting the Kingdoms of Sicily and Naples (ruled by the French Bourbons) with the Kingdom of Sardinia (ruled by the State of Savoy), the protagonist, who belongs to the old ruling class, adopts a pragmatic attitude by persuading his Sicilian people to lend support to the new democratic regime. He also encourages his ambitious nephew Tancredi in his scheme to marry Angelica, the dazzling daughter of a nouveau riche from the peasant class, even though he is aware that his youngest daughter is madly in love with Tancredi.<br /><br />Much of the novel is devoted to exhibiting Fabrizio’s emotional torment, moral struggles and psychological turmoil that arise from the unsettling external paradigm shift, signifying the inexorable demise of an era, to which he is irredeemably attached.<br /><br />Perhaps this passage reveals the essence of Fabrizio’s pride and agony:<br /><br /><i>It was useless to try to avoid the thought, but the last of the Salinas was really he himself, this gaunt giant now dying on a hotel balcony. For the significance of a noble family lies entirely in its traditions, that is in its vital memories; and he was the last to have any unusual memories, anything different from those of other families.</i> <br /><br />I’m giving this novel 3.8 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1964628621349879757" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3391097573" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3391097573" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3391097573?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=european-lit">european-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3391097573" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7156844651360532437">In the wake of Risorgimento (Italian Unification), the aristocratic nobility was threatened with the possible decline of their feudal power. They had to contemplate a future where their power was ripped off and passed on to the commoners who have now acquired wealth and power almost equal to them. The change is inevitable yet is it easy to face it? Could the proud nobles mix themselves with the commoners thus depriving them of their elite status or was this necessary to preserve their wealth, th</span>
  <span id="freeText7156844651360532437" style="display:none">In the wake of Risorgimento (Italian Unification), the aristocratic nobility was threatened with the possible decline of their feudal power. They had to contemplate a future where their power was ripped off and passed on to the commoners who have now acquired wealth and power almost equal to them. The change is inevitable yet is it easy to face it? Could the proud nobles mix themselves with the commoners thus depriving them of their elite status or was this necessary to preserve their wealth, their power and authority in albeit an altered way? These are the questions Lampedusa tries to address in his semi-autobiographic work - <i> The Leopard</i>.<br /><br />Don Fabrizio, the Prince of Salina whose power and authority is threatened comprehends that if they are to survive they must move with the flow. His dictum is <i>" everything must change so that everything must stay the same"</i>. However unwelcome and irksome the change is, it is necessary, and this the Prince understands. Yet it is not easy to suppress the years of noble pride, to reduce them to the equal status of the newly rich and powerful commoners. Don Fabriozi struggles to reconcile himself with these new realities a little consciously but more unconsciously. However, he doesn't waver from his dictum and consents his penniless nephew Tancredi, the Prince of Falconeri, to marry the daughter of a common unsophisticated wealthy man who wealds power in the new regime. Don Fabriozi's design is that his young and ambitious nephew should climb up in the social and political ladder and help preserve some form of authority to the Selina family. To achieve this end, Tancredi needs money - a lot of it. Therefore this otherwise socially unthinkable and undesirable match has to be encouraged and made. But even so, though he admires the beautiful girl who would soon be his niece, he cannot help the utter loathing he feels for her commoner wealthy father. Don Fabrizio is the leopard (the official symbol of the House of Salina) - strong and powerful yet also graceful. How the proud leopard is to live equally with the "jackals and hyenas" who have usurped the leopard's power and authority is Don Fabriozi's dilemma. And his inner struggle to come to terms with this new predicament flows beautifully throughout the story. But Don Fabriozi's struggle doesn't end there. He has to fight against another more formidable change, and that is the change in himself as he slowly nears death.<br /><br />Change and how to adapt to the change is the key theme of the story. Don Fabrizio accommodates the change grudgingly because it is inevitable and also because it is necessary. But the young Tancredi, also of noble blood, yields more easily and voluntarily. He has no difficulty in joining hands with the revolutionaries and taking part in the revolution. The change comes more naturally to him. Lampedusa quite intelligently draws the contrast between the attitudes of the younger and older generation of the nobility. <br /><br /><i> The Leopard</i> brings us some memorable characters. But the unique and dominating is Don Fabrizio, the sensual, the powerful, the graceful "leopard". Lampedusa's poetic writing, subtle symbology, and suitable satire narrate a heartfelt story of an end of an era. I didn't know what to expect when I began the reading. There was a lot of history behind the story that I was compelled to read up. But it was quite worth it, for the book was very rewarding.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7156844651360532437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_368349703" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/368349703" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/368349703?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=european-novels">european-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer368349703" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11460656197192303690">A rich and luscious novel about a decaying aristocratic family in nineteenth century Sicily. The main protagonists are the Salina family and especially Don Fabrizio (the Leopard of the title) the head of the family. Most of the novel takes place in the early 1860s and there is great descriptive detail throughout capturing the heat and dust of the Sicilian countryside. Lampedusa's descriptions of scents and smells and a decaying grand house are sublime. Religion and the ritual of the Catholic chu</span>
  <span id="freeText11460656197192303690" style="display:none">A rich and luscious novel about a decaying aristocratic family in nineteenth century Sicily. The main protagonists are the Salina family and especially Don Fabrizio (the Leopard of the title) the head of the family. Most of the novel takes place in the early 1860s and there is great descriptive detail throughout capturing the heat and dust of the Sicilian countryside. Lampedusa's descriptions of scents and smells and a decaying grand house are sublime. Religion and the ritual of the Catholic church runs throughout the book as a theme and backdrop, dominating some characters. The events leading up to the unification of Italy and the exploits of Garibaldi are also part of the background and illustrate how the aristocracy adapted to the new order.Don Fabrizio dominates the book and his thoughts and feelings about the advent of modernity and the idionsyncracies of his rather repressed family and subtly and cleverly expressed. The other strand of the plot is the courtship and eventual marraige of Tancredi (Don Fabrizio's nephew) and the beautiful Angelica. There is a delicious passage where the courting couple are exploring the palace of Don Fabrizio at Donnafugata. There are hundreds of empty and abandoned rooms, not used for years. they stumble into the playroom of a libertine (probably eighteenth century). The walls are covered in mirrors, some broken, strategically placed beds and several whips (50 shades of Sicilian Grey no doubt). Nearby they find another room; smaller and much more sparse. There is a cross on the wall and I think a prie-dieux for prayer. On the wall is another whip; for self flagellation during prayer; only this time there are small lead balls fixed into the ends of the leather thongs; the church is so much more imaginative when it comes to pain and bondage (50 shades darker?)<br />The last two chapters change the tone. We move to the 1880s and the death bed of Don Fabrizio. As death bed scenes go this one isn't bad; but the cliches of a generally peaceful death with family around are still there; as is the mandatory priest to pronounce the last rites. The sand running out description is interesting because it is taken to a different level and the fading of the noise in the room is cleverly described. The aloneness in a crowd feeling of the dying man is a little reminiscent of Beckett in Malone Dies. It is certainly different to Dickens' death bed scenes (I think it was Wilde who said that anyone who could read the death bed scene of Little Nell (Old Curiosity Shop) without laughing has to have a heart of stone). Having been at a number of death beds as a result of two of my former occupations; there is a little accuracy here. For a better description of death beds see Keizer's excellent Dancing with Mister D. However in this novel it works well.<br />The last chapter takes us into the early twentieth century and Don Fabrizio's three unmarried daughters are still living in increasingly decaying circumstances; the gradual destruction of a way of life is almost complete.<br />Worth a read; it captures a long gone way of life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11460656197192303690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_635466140" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/635466140" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/635466140?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer635466140" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer325133798326560896">Unlike in many other novels of historical fiction, Tomasi makes no secret of the fact that he is writing from the vantage of hindsight. And though they were few, I enjoyed his narratorial asides, some ironic, some sobering. But what I loved more than anything else is the elegant writing; you are in a dream as the sentences flow by. Two sections stand out as especially beautiful: the young couple playing amongst the closed-off ruins of rooms in the palace and the main character facing death -- su</span>
  <span id="freeText325133798326560896" style="display:none">Unlike in many other novels of historical fiction, Tomasi makes no secret of the fact that he is writing from the vantage of hindsight. And though they were few, I enjoyed his narratorial asides, some ironic, some sobering. But what I loved more than anything else is the elegant writing; you are in a dream as the sentences flow by. Two sections stand out as especially beautiful: the young couple playing amongst the closed-off ruins of rooms in the palace and the main character facing death -- such haunting, effective images throughout! The ending rests on one of my favorite themes as <i>a new layer of soil fell on the tumulus of truth.</i><br /><br />I am also pleased to have finally read this as my books-read list is lacking in Italian writers. This is especially egregious considering I am of Sicilian descent. Seriously, could <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/83015.The_Bears__Famous_Invasion_of_Sicily" title="The Bears' Famous Invasion of Sicily by Dino Buzzati" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bears' Famous Invasion of Sicily</a> be the only other book by an Italian author that I've read?!<br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>P.S.</b> And the answer to my sort-of rhetorical question above is a resounding <b>NO</b>. Due to a memory jog by Michael (see comments 18 and 20), I realized that I have read more Italian writers than I originally thought!</span>
  <a data-text-id="325133798326560896" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2979786786" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2979786786" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31384949-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584889764p2/31384949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2979786786?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/31384949-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11546057-the-leopard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=4-star-lit-fiction">4-star-lit-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=italy">italy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2979786786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11704136867037527485">Although this is a very famous novel I’d not personally heard of it until I joined GR in 2014. Over the past 5 years I’ve seen various reviews of it, always positive, and so was persuaded to try it.<br/><br/>As is well-known, the bulk of the novel is set in Sicily in the 1860s. The central character, Prince Fabrizio Salina, exercises a paternalistic rule over the tenants of his vast estates, across a Sicily that seems timeless but which is now faced with change, or, as the Prince views it, with decay. The</span>
  <span id="freeText11704136867037527485" style="display:none">Although this is a very famous novel I’d not personally heard of it until I joined GR in 2014. Over the past 5 years I’ve seen various reviews of it, always positive, and so was persuaded to try it.<br /><br />As is well-known, the bulk of the novel is set in Sicily in the 1860s. The central character, Prince Fabrizio Salina, exercises a paternalistic rule over the tenants of his vast estates, across a Sicily that seems timeless but which is now faced with change, or, as the Prince views it, with decay. The combination of Italian unification and the rise of a new commercial class threatens to overwhelm the traditional political and economic roles of the island’s ossified aristocracy, and the Prince realises that his family will have to adapt to the changes. A subplot featuring the Salina family priest, Father Pirrone, provides the reader with an insight into Sicilian peasant society. <br /><br />I could only read the English translation, but it seems a high quality one, conveying a fine sense of the richness of the writing. It says much for the author’s skill that he makes a feudal aristocrat like Don Fabrizio such a sympathetic figure to the modern reader. For me, the dominant theme of the novel is one of sadness, one might say hopelessness. <br /><br />Over the last couple of weeks I’ve not had as much time for reading as I would have liked. This rather broke up my enjoyment of the book. It’s one I might return to one day.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11704136867037527485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_388306815" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/388306815" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/388306815?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=italian-literature">italian-literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388306815" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2363450096652009402">I tremendously enjoyed reading this book. The first chapter is a bit difficult to read because it was left unfinished by the author and had to be posthumously composed from unfinished material (by Giorgio Bassani nota bene). But then the Leopard turns into a lovely 19th century society novel full of psychology, awesome landscapes and social realism. You not only get a beautiful description of Sicily and the Sicilians in the middle of the 19th Century, it is above all a study in political and soc</span>
  <span id="freeText2363450096652009402" style="display:none">I tremendously enjoyed reading this book. The first chapter is a bit difficult to read because it was left unfinished by the author and had to be posthumously composed from unfinished material (by Giorgio Bassani nota bene). But then the Leopard turns into a lovely 19th century society novel full of psychology, awesome landscapes and social realism. You not only get a beautiful description of Sicily and the Sicilians in the middle of the 19th Century, it is above all a study in political and social shifts and how people cope with them. Don Fabrizio, the towering aristocrat must give way to his upcoming, modern but short-sighted bourgeois neighbour, and he does so with pain in his heart, but also with a combination of very pragmatic fatalism and some slight cunning ("<i>everything must change, so that everything can stay the same</i>"). <br />I cannot but recommend this book, and also the (very long and slow) film (1963) by Visconti with a fabulous Burt Lancaster remains impressive!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2363450096652009402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1218172605" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1218172605" />
    <a title="[P]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40516522-p"><img alt="[P]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486240560p2/40516522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1218172605?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="[P]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="[P]" href="/user/show/40516522-p">[P]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40516522-p?shelf=bitchin">bitchin</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1218172605" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15377971829404926523">The other day I found a grey hair, by which I mean on my own head, of course, not on the floor. If I was in my forties, or upwards, I may have anticipated such a thing, but, in my naivety, I didn’t think it possible at my age. Yet there it was, gesturing to me in an offensive manner; it was like staring at a crowd of people and suddenly spotting, deep in their midst, a child looking my way and insouciantly giving me the finger. I’ve been, it is fair to say, somewhat perturbed ever since; I keep </span>
  <span id="freeText15377971829404926523" style="display:none">The other day I found a grey hair, by which I mean on my own head, of course, not on the floor. If I was in my forties, or upwards, I may have anticipated such a thing, but, in my naivety, I didn’t think it possible at my age. Yet there it was, gesturing to me in an offensive manner; it was like staring at a crowd of people and suddenly spotting, deep in their midst, a child looking my way and insouciantly giving me the finger. I’ve been, it is fair to say, somewhat perturbed ever since; I keep checking the backs of my hands, and around my eyes, for signs of wrinkles, and any slight twinge or ache strikes me as the inevitable, irrevocable, breaking down of my mechanism. This is, and always has been, my worst fear. Decline, old age, and their tyrannical father: death. How on earth do you face up to that? You haven’t got much of a choice, I guess. How awful! Some people are blasé about it; ‘it’s fine,’ they say, ‘ageing is a positive thing; ‘I’m not afraid of death,’ they say, ‘I’m more concerned about how I will go.’ I’ve never understood all that. I’m don’t care one bit about the manner of my death, it’s the fact that it is going to happen at all that bothers me; it’s the not-being that terrifies me. ‘But wouldn’t it be terrible to be immortal, to remain young, while all your loved ones, your family and friends, age and pass away?’ No, it’d be glorious! Make no mistake I’d gaily skip down the street as the last man on earth.<br /><br />There have been many fine novels about all of this – Samuel Beckett, for example, wrote reams of them – but I think my favourite is <i>Il Gattopardo</i>, or <i>The Leopard</i>, by Giuseppe Tomasi di Lampedusa. In the opening pages of the novel, we are introduced to Don Fabrizio, the Sicilian aristocrat who dominates the book. The imposing, heavy-set, Prince of Salina is an old-fashioned sort, conservative in values where his family are concerned, but more than willing to give himself major leeway. For example, he demands the utmost respect and propriety from his children, and yet brazenly cheats on his wife and, on one occasion, drags Father Pirrone along on one of his amorous escapades, almost as a display of his power. The children are, of course, petrified of him; it is noted that the household cutlery has had to be straightened numerous times, for their father, in moments of anger or irritation, has a tendency to grasp knifes and forks and spoons in his heavy paws and bend them.<br /><br />In contrast to his outward displays of strength, the domineering Fabrizio is, privately, prone to melancholy and self-pity. He may rule his children and wife with the proverbial iron fist, but this does not stop them from disappointing him; in fact, almost everything disappoints him. His son, Paolo, is referred to as a ‘booby,’ and is less than favourably compared with the Prince’s nephew, Tancredi. Fabrizio appears to have more affection for his daughter, Concetta, but even she frequently irritates him, and is, sadly, no match, in terms of looks, for Angelica Sedara, the daughter of a nouveau riche Mayor, whom Tancredi wishes to marry. His wife, on the other hand, is a woman of strained nerves, who is no longer sexually alluring to him; indeed, her pious reserve [Fabrizio claims to have never seen her navel] is used as justification for infidelity. This disappointment also extends to himself, or at least his own mortality, and to the state of the country.<br /><br /><i>The Leopard</i> is set in the years 1860-1862, 1883 and 1910, during a period in history known as the Risorgimento, the aim of which was the unification of Italy. It was, then, a time of revolution, change, and unrest. On this basis, one could legitimately call <i>The Leopard</i> a political novel, but the politics feed into the broader and, for me, more important and engaging themes of decline and death. In the most literal way, war or revolution drag death and destruction in their wake, of course, and this is brought into sharp focus when the mutilated body of a soldier is found in the Prince’s garden. But what the Risorgimento really represents, what it brings home to Don Fabrizio, is that the old ways, his ways, are numbered. Indeed, one of the aims of the Risorgimento was a levelling of the classes, so while the rich and powerful Don Fabrizio is not directly involved in the conflict his kind are, in a way, a target, and therefore they are, culturally-socially, on borrowed time.<br /><br /><blockquote>“We were the Leopards, the Lions; those who’ll take our place will be little jackals, hyenas; and the whole lot of us, Leopards, jackals, and sheep, we’ll all go on thinking ourselves the salt of the earth.”</blockquote><br /><br />Unfortunately, the Prince, like all of us, is also on borrowed time physically. One of the most impressive aspects of the novel is how the decline of the old ways of Italy are actually mirrored in Fabrizio’s own personal decline; the two reflect each other. Despite being only in his forties, from the very beginning there is a sense that the Prince is no longer at his peak, that he is not staring proudly from the heights of physical perfection, but is steadily making his way back down the mountain. For example, Don Fabrizio’s sensual-sexual nature is frequently alluded to; as noted, he cheats on his wife, and he is struck by, and excited by, Angelica’s attractions [her beauty, her body, etc]. However, he is also fully aware that he is no longer in the running, so to speak, that the vibrant young woman will prefer the charming, and also young, Tancredi. This is not the same, alas, as saying that he is happy about it. Far from it; he feels, rather, a twinge of jealousy, a sexual jealousy that is not particularly admirable, of course, but is understandable.<br /><br /><blockquote>“To kneel before Angelica would be a pleasure, but what if he found it difficult to get up afterwards?”</blockquote><br /><br />To say that the Prince is not as vital as he once was, and that Italy is at war and going through important social-political changes, does not do justice to how deeply ingrained the book’s preoccupations and themes are. I said before that it is perhaps the greatest novel about death and decline, and to understand this one must read it, because these things are present in the text on almost every page. Indeed, Lampedusa’s work is so rich in allusions and references to them that the atmosphere is of a unrelenting gloominess, almost regardless of the main narrated action. For example, it is at one point noted that the Prince’s initials on a wine glass have begun to fade; Bendico, his dog, noses his way through the garden smelling of ‘dead lizards and manure’; Fabrizio goes hunting at Donnafugata, but hardly ever shoots anything, because there are scant targets; as payment for rent he is given slaughtered lambs, stories are shared about poisoned holy water and people cut up into little pieces, and so on.<br /><br /><blockquote>“As always the thought of his own death calmed him as much as that of others disturbed him: was it perhaps because, when all was said and done, his own death would in the first place mean that of the whole world?”</blockquote><br /><br />Giuseppe Tomasi di Lampedusa, himself a Sicilian aristocrat, only ever wrote one novel; and even this was rejected numerous times and was published only after his own death. If I had to guess as to why it wasn’t instantly appreciated I would perhaps point to the intricate, detailed prose as being something of an acquired taste. Don’t get me wrong, I absolutely love it; in fact, I consider Lampedusa to be one of the very finest prose stylists; his extended metaphors alone make reading the book worthwhile. But it is decidedly Proustian, perhaps more so than any other that gets lumbered with that tag, and his prose, by which I mean Marcel’s, is also an acquired taste [it seems]. Moreover, Lampedusa’s novel lacks the emotional sturm and drang of certain parts of <i>In Search of Lost Time</i>, is just not as viscerally exciting as, say, <i>Sodom and Gomorrah</i>. <i>The Leopard</i> is a slow book, a deeply ruminative book, with very little action. It is, the author himself claimed, not very good. He was wrong, of course; it’s a masterpiece. Now if you’ll excuse me I’m going to listen to Frank Sinatra’s <i>It Was a Very Good Year</i>, and then go quietly weep in a corner.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15377971829404926523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_50725024" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50725024" />
    <a title="Ademption" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1045093-ademption"><img alt="Ademption" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1264349089p2/1045093.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50725024?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ademption" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ademption" href="/user/show/1045093-ademption">Ademption</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">sinister adults</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1045093-ademption?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1045093-ademption?shelf=italian-literature">italian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1045093-ademption?shelf=novels">novels</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50725024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10882897714966831376">The quintessence of melancholy, <i>The Leopard</i>, lets the reader try on the skin of the titular character: the last prince in a declining aristocracy. It reminded me of <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31072.Under_the_Volcano' title='Under the Volcano by Malcolm Lowry' rel='nofollow noopener'>Under the Volcano</a>
</i>. I was pushed to empathize with the last leonine lord of Sicily as intimately as I did with the alcoholic diplomat in <i>Under the Volcano</i>, despite never having aspirations towards being crowned or pickled. Both novels deal with cornered people doing their best while their world turns to dust. <i>The Leopard</i> is beauti</span>
  <span id="freeText10882897714966831376" style="display:none">The quintessence of melancholy, <i>The Leopard</i>, lets the reader try on the skin of the titular character: the last prince in a declining aristocracy. It reminded me of <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31072.Under_the_Volcano" title="Under the Volcano by Malcolm Lowry" rel="nofollow noopener">Under the Volcano</a>
</i>. I was pushed to empathize with the last leonine lord of Sicily as intimately as I did with the alcoholic diplomat in <i>Under the Volcano</i>, despite never having aspirations towards being crowned or pickled. Both novels deal with cornered people doing their best while their world turns to dust. <i>The Leopard</i> is beautiful, and darkly funny, while Lowry's novel is from a much harsher cast of gorgeousness (Think: The Smiths vs. Placebo). I also found it comforting that the Leopard had the same complaints and human afflictions we all do. All the relationships, affairs, and finest products in the world do nothing to cure him of falling short of an ideal. It gave me a sense of peace about my place in the world and in history. Nothing will be left of me a century after my death, and that is freeing as fuck. All the worry that comes from ambition won't cure me from the human condition. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10882897714966831376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Whitaker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1415047-whitaker"><img alt="Whitaker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410926609p2/1415047.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11546057-the-leopard">review of another edition</a>

            Shelves:

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1415047-whitaker?shelf=italy">italy</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer250396296" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8037285354470690831">It is no coincidence that <i>The Leopard</i> is bookended by two corpses: a decomposing one at the beginning, and an embalmed one at the end. The middle is filled with the story of a third corpse whose slow decomposition and putrefaction make up the meat of the novel. Rigour mortis first sets in, as traditions rigidify the body. It gets devoured internally, its body bloating, consumed by its own bacteria—the peasants that require the pacification and gifts demanded by noblesse oblige, the expensive pom</span>
  <span id="freeText8037285354470690831" style="display:none">It is no coincidence that <i>The Leopard</i> is bookended by two corpses: a decomposing one at the beginning, and an embalmed one at the end. The middle is filled with the story of a third corpse whose slow decomposition and putrefaction make up the meat of the novel. Rigour mortis first sets in, as traditions rigidify the body. It gets devoured internally, its body bloating, consumed by its own bacteria—the peasants that require the pacification and gifts demanded by noblesse oblige, the expensive pomp and ceremony demanded of membership in the aristocracy. Gradually, slowly, the corpse is consumed by blowflies and maggots—the nouveau riche who buy off its properties at fire sale prices. Finally, in a final transformation, new vegetation sprouts from the rich soil of the decomposed body, new growth that will in its turn die and be devoured in the eternal transformation of societal evolution. All the while, the sweet stench of decay mingles with the heady odour of beauty and memories of a more vigorous and virile youth. Lampedusa captures it all in ambivalently lambent language, the beauty really of deadly nightshade or angel’s trumpet. Lovely. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380365465i/717541.jpg" width="350" height="364" alt="purple deadly nightshade" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380365465i/717542.jpg" width="350" height="278" alt="pink angel's trumpet" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13536391667251607999"><i>“Love. Of course, love. Flames for a year, ashes for thirty.”</i><br/><br/>The exquisite story of a man, a place, an ancient society when it all changes; or doesn’t. Written by the last Prince of Lampedusa about the life of an ancestor and published posthumously, it is everything I adore about Italy: delicious, sensuous and preposterous. Irony so carefully wrought, so thoughtfully carved, so fragile. <br/><br/>The Prince of the story accepts, loathes, and loves his Sicily in equal measure.<br/><i>“In Sicily it doesn't matter w</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13536391667251607999" style="display:none"><i>“Love. Of course, love. Flames for a year, ashes for thirty.”</i><br /><br />The exquisite story of a man, a place, an ancient society when it all changes; or doesn’t. Written by the last Prince of Lampedusa about the life of an ancestor and published posthumously, it is everything I adore about Italy: delicious, sensuous and preposterous. Irony so carefully wrought, so thoughtfully carved, so fragile. <br /><br />The Prince of the story accepts, loathes, and loves his Sicily in equal measure.<br /><i>“In Sicily it doesn't matter whether things are done well or done badly; the sin which we Sicilians never forgive is simply that of ‘doing’ at all.”<br /> “...sleep, that is what Sicilians want, and they will always hate anyone who tries to wake them, even in order to bring them the most wonderful of gifts…”</i><br />He describes the <br /><i>“... hankering for voluptuous immobility”</i> and I find myself wanting to nap under a tree in the Mediterranean sun. <br /><br />Or maybe just appreciate the efforts of The Dog; whose work is never done: <br /><i>“He was sitting on a bench, inertly watching the devastation wrought by Bendico in the flower beds; every now and again the dog would turn innocent eyes toward him as if asking for praise at labor done: fourteen carnations broken off, half a hedge torn apart, an irrigation canal blocked. How human! “Good! Bendico, come here.” And the animal hurried up and put its earthy nostrils into his hand, anxious to show that it had forgiven this silly interruption of a fine job of work.”</i><br /><br />Arriving with his family (after Garibaldi’s victory over Sicily) at a country estate in the small, rural town of Donnafugata:<br /><i> “...they moved there in procession, the new arrivals dusty but imposing, the authorities gleaming but humble.”</i><br />Our Prince, The Leopard, then extends an unprecedented opening to the People, with predictable (?) results:<br /><i>“ And he added, turning to the others, “And after dinner, at nine o’clock, we shall be happy to see all our friends.” For a long time Donnafugata commented on these last words. And the Prince, who had found Donnafugata unchanged, was found very much changed himself, for never before would he have issued so cordial an invitation; and from that moment, invisibly, began the decline of his prestige.”</i><br /><br />Full of understated wit and brilliant circular logic which discolors, or illuminates, truth in all of its malleability.<br /><br />A priest explaining ‘nobles’:<br /><i>“They’re just different; perhaps they appear so strange to us because they have reached a stage toward which all those who are not saints are moving, that of indifference to worldly goods through surfeit.”<br />“...when they treat someone badly, as they do sometimes, it is not so much their personality sinning as their class affirming itself.”<br /> “...if, as has often happened before, this class were to vanish, an equivalent one would be formed straight away with the same qualities and the same defects; it might not be based on blood anymore, but possibly on...on, say, the length of time lived in a place, or on greater knowledge of some text considered sacred.”</i><br />And i, recalling Lapham’s <u>Money and Class in America </u>, think - or on cruelty and cash - as in the USA?<br /><br /><i>“From the ceiling the gods, reclining on gilded couches, gazed down smiling and inexorable as a summer sky. They thought themselves eternal; but a bomb manufactured in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania, was to prove the contrary in 1943.”</i><br /><br />ad maius bonum gentis.<br />utique.</span>

<div id="review_1014278261" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1014278261" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1014278261?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1014278261" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14382445022471707691">Italian writers tend to have a close affinity with the location their novel is set in; Morante’s ‘History’ is just as much about war-torn Rome as it is about the characters, Delleda’s ‘Reeds in the Wind’ is imbued with the atmosphere of Sardinia, Levi’s ‘The Periodic Table’ is entwined with the Piedmont of Levi’s youth and ‘The Garden of the Finzi-Cortinis recreates 1930’s Ferrara perfectly. Yet no Italian novel encapsulates the environment it is set in like ‘The Leopard’.Bucolic and beautiful, </span>
  <span id="freeText14382445022471707691" style="display:none">Italian writers tend to have a close affinity with the location their novel is set in; Morante’s ‘History’ is just as much about war-torn Rome as it is about the characters, Delleda’s ‘Reeds in the Wind’ is imbued with the atmosphere of Sardinia, Levi’s ‘The Periodic Table’ is entwined with the Piedmont of Levi’s youth and ‘The Garden of the Finzi-Cortinis recreates 1930’s Ferrara perfectly. Yet no Italian novel encapsulates the environment it is set in like ‘The Leopard’.Bucolic and beautiful, Lampedusa captures the multifarious facets of Scilly, from the stifling heat to the shimmer of sunlight on the barren wastelands which dominate Scilly to the bounteous beauty of the villa gardens; <br /><br />“The sun was already low and its rays; no longer over-whelming were lighting amiably on the araucarias, the pines, the lusty ilexes which were the glory of the place. The water came spurting in minute jets, blown from shells of Tritons and Naiads, from noses of marine monsters, spattering nad pattering on the greenish surface, bouncng and bubbling, wavering and quivering, dissolving into laughing little gurgles, from the whole fountain, the tepid water, the stones covered with velvety moss, emanated a pleasure which would never turn to pain”<br /><br /> Lampedusa’s Sicily reverberates under the weight of the novel’s protaganist, the Prince of Salina, who lumbers lugubriously about the novel, swept along to drown in the swift torrents of time as the world of genteel aristocracy and the Bourbon regime is replaced by the crude, populist designs if Italian risorgimento. ‘The Leopard’ is not a political story-although the story hovers on the edges of the political upheaval in which the story is set-instead it is a study of the Sicilian society in the 1860’s, of Italian machismo, of the irascible and forbidding Prince and his family-the proud and haughty Concetta, his long-suffering wife Donna Maria and his handsome, jocular nephew Tancredia-the characters may read like those from an Italian soap opera, yet the Lampedusa is able to bring them to life, recreate their dreams and desires and ultimately disappointments. ‘The Leopard’ is steeped in pessimism, the titular Prince is perpetually wallowing in existential angst, whose sole relief from the emptiness of life seems to be star-gazing. Lampedusa is, of course, writing the story from the point-of-view of a man in the twilight of his life, who has tasted all the pleasures that life has to offer, yet the sense of pessimism haunts all the characters-from the sad, spinisterish state of the Prince’s daughters, to the vague, vapid vageries of Tancredi, a sense of emptiness and disillusion permeates the novel.<br /><br />Yet Lampedusa’s ability to capture the different facets of Sicilian life, from the arduous and ignorant lives of the peasantry to the privileged yet spiritually empty lives of the aristocracy means ‘The Leopard’ is unrivalled as a historical study of 19th century Sicily. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14382445022471707691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Oio8dTWYGyf7vd0gP3odH3nOr+GUandvyWVRJ0OcJbwADFWnjsbST1yWaUHIjxfAr5FIKVrn+QiSGxeWjt/XJg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Avvertendo come poco espressive le realtà borghesi, Verga<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17051538119000859924\" style=\"display:none\">Nei Malavoglia (1881) Verga si immerge nella realtà locale di un paese siciliano, Aci Trezza, e racconta la semplice e rissosa quotidianità in cui vivono i Toscano, detti appunto i Malavoglia. Avvertendo come poco espressive le realtà borghesi, Verga cerca di rompere l\'impianto romanzesco classico, reinventando le regole del gioco narrativo per dare spazio alla coralità dei personaggi, ritratti nella loro vitale specificità. Questa edizione mette in luce la storia e la formazione del testo attraverso i successivi progetti e abbozzi dell\'autore. Banished from hi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4842797123941447809\" style=\"display:none\">Written as an epistolary monologue, Last Letters of Jacopo Ortis is a compelling portrayal of a troubled mind. Published here for the first time in the English language, it is presented with Foscolo\'s highly acclaimed poem, Of Tombs. Banished from his homeland and from the woman he loves, Jacopo Ortis lives with the insufferable feelings of disillusionment and betrayal. Gone are his youthful dreams of literary glory, and in their place only his embittered laughter at fortune, at men, and at God. In the anguish of his state he feels himself compelled to make one final, titanic, and tragic gesture to the rulers of his age.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4842797123941447809\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17246729226626249737">Tomasi was born in Palermo to Giulio Maria Tomasi, Prince of Lampedusa and Duke of Palma di Montechiaro, and Beatrice Mastrogiovanni Tasca Filangieri di Cutò. He became an only child after the death (from diphtheria) of his sister. He was very close to his mother, a strong personality who influenced him a great deal, especially because his father was rather cold and detached. As a child he studied</span>
  <span id="freeText17246729226626249737" style="display:none">Tomasi was born in Palermo to Giulio Maria Tomasi, Prince of Lampedusa and Duke of Palma di Montechiaro, and Beatrice Mastrogiovanni Tasca Filangieri di Cutò. He became an only child after the death (from diphtheria) of his sister. He was very close to his mother, a strong personality who influenced him a great deal, especially because his father was rather cold and detached. As a child he studied in their grand house in Palermo with a tutor (including the subjects of literature and English), with his mother (who taught him French), and with a grandmother who read him the novels of Emilio Salgari. In the little theater of the house in Santa Margherita di Belice, where he spent long vacations, he first saw a performance of Shakespeare's Hamlet, performed by a company of travelling players. His cousin was Fulco di Verdura.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17246729226626249737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
